<p><b>Troy</b> (<a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">Œ§œÅŒøŒØŒ±</span>, <i>Tro√≠a</i>, <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">·ººŒªŒπŒøŒΩ</span>, <i>√çlion</i> or  <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">·ººŒªŒπŒøœÇ</span>, <i>√çlios</i>; <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Troia</i> and <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Ilium</i>;<sup id="cite_ref-ilium_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ilium-1">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hittite_language" title="Hittite language">Hittite</a>: íå∑íÉæíáªíä≠ <i>Wilusa</i> or íã´íäííÑøíä≠ <i>Truwisa</i>;<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Burney2004_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burney2004-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>: <i lang="tr">Truva or Troya</i>) was a city in the far northwest of the region known in late <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Classical antiquity</a> as <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Asia Minor</a>, now known as <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> in modern <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, just south of the southwest mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Dardanelles" title="Dardanelles">Dardanelles</a> <a href="/wiki/Strait" title="Strait">strait</a> and northwest of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Ida,_Turkey" class="mw-redirect" title="Mount Ida, Turkey">Mount Ida</a>. The present-day location is known as <a href="/wiki/Hisarlik" title="Hisarlik">Hisarlik</a>. It was the setting of the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_War" title="Trojan War">Trojan War</a> described in the Greek <a href="/wiki/Epic_Cycle" title="Epic Cycle">Epic Cycle</a>, in particular in the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i>, one of the two epic poems attributed to <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>. <a href="/wiki/Metre_(poetry)" title="Metre (poetry)">Metrical</a> evidence from the <i>Iliad</i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i> suggests that the name <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">·ººŒªŒπŒøŒΩ</span> (<i>Ilion</i>) formerly began with a <a href="/wiki/Digamma" title="Digamma">digamma</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">œúŒØŒªŒπŒøŒΩ</span> (<i>Wilion</i>); this is also supported by the <a href="/wiki/Hittite_language" title="Hittite language">Hittite</a> name for what is thought to be the same city, <a href="/wiki/Wilusa" title="Wilusa">Wilusa</a>.
</p><p>A new capital called <b>Ilium</b> (from Greek: ·ººŒªŒπŒøŒΩ, <i>Ilion</i>) was founded on the site in the reign of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Emperor">Roman Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a>. It flourished until the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, became a bishopric and declined gradually in the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine era</a>, but is now a Latin Catholic <a href="/wiki/Titular_see" title="Titular see">titular see</a>.
</p><p>In 1865, English archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Frank_Calvert" title="Frank Calvert">Frank Calvert</a> excavated trial trenches in a field he had bought from a local farmer at Hisarlik, and in 1868, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Schliemann" title="Heinrich Schliemann">Heinrich Schliemann</a>, a wealthy German businessman and archaeologist, also began excavating in the area after a chance meeting with Calvert in <a href="/wiki/%C3%87anakkale" title="√áanakkale">√áanakkale</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> These excavations revealed several cities built in succession. Schliemann was at first skeptical about the identification of Hisarlik with Troy, but was persuaded by Calvert<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and took over Calvert's excavations on the eastern half of the Hisarlik site, which was on Calvert's property. <a href="/wiki/Troy_VII" title="Troy VII">Troy VII</a> has been identified with the city called Wilusa by the Hittites (the probable origin of the Greek ·ººŒªŒπŒøŒΩ) and is generally (but not conclusively) identified with Homeric Troy.
</p><p>Today, the hill at Hisarlik has given its name to a small village near the ruins, which supports the tourist trade visiting the Troia archaeological site.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> It lies within the <a href="/wiki/%C3%87anakkale_Province" title="√áanakkale Province">province of √áanakkale</a>, some 30&#160;km south-west of the provincial capital, also called √áanakkale. The nearest village is Tevfikiye. The map here shows the adapted Scamander estuary with Ilium a little way inland across the Homeric plain. Due to Troy's location near the Aegean Sea, the Sea of Marmara, and the Black Sea, it was a central hub for the military and trade.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Troy was added to the <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage</a> list in 1998.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Homeric_Troy">Homeric Troy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Homeric Troy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Homeric_Question" title="Homeric Question">Homeric Question</a>, <a href="/wiki/Historicity_of_the_Iliad" class="mw-redirect" title="Historicity of the Iliad">Historicity of the Iliad</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Troy_VII" title="Troy VII">Troy VII</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Walls_of_Troy_(1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Walls_of_Troy_%281%29.jpg/220px-Walls_of_Troy_%281%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Walls_of_Troy_%281%29.jpg/330px-Walls_of_Troy_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Walls_of_Troy_%281%29.jpg/440px-Walls_of_Troy_%281%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1454" data-file-height="1090" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Walls_of_Troy_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portion of the walls of Troy (VII)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Troas.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Troas.png/220px-Troas.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Troas.png/330px-Troas.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Troas.png/440px-Troas.png 2x" data-file-width="520" data-file-height="539" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Troas.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Troad, including the site of Troy</div></div></div>
<p>Ancient Greek historians variously placed the Trojan War in the 12th, 13th, or 14th centuries BC: <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a> to 1184&#160;BC, <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> to 1250&#160;BC, and <a href="/wiki/Duris_of_Samos" title="Duris of Samos">Duris of Samos</a> to 1334&#160;BC. Modern archaeologists associate Homeric Troy with archaeological <a href="/wiki/Troy_VII" title="Troy VII">Troy VII</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i>Iliad</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Achaeans_(Homer)" title="Achaeans (Homer)">Achaeans</a> set up their camp near the mouth of the River <a href="/wiki/Scamander" title="Scamander">Scamander</a> (presumably modern <a href="/wiki/Karamenderes" class="mw-redirect" title="Karamenderes">Karamenderes</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-ShopesHamilton2008_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ShopesHamilton2008-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> where they beached their ships. The city of Troy itself stood on a hill, across the plain of Scamander, where the battles of the Trojan War took place. The site of the ancient city is some 5&#160;km from the coast today, but 3,000 years ago the mouths of Scamander were much closer to the city,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> discharging into a large bay that formed a natural harbor, which has since been filled with <a href="/wiki/Alluvial" class="mw-redirect" title="Alluvial">alluvial</a> material. Recent geological findings have permitted the identification of the ancient Trojan coastline, and the results largely confirm the accuracy of the Homeric geography of Troy.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2001, the geologist John C. Kraft from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Delaware" title="University of Delaware">University of Delaware</a> and the classicist <a href="/wiki/John_V._Luce" title="John V. Luce">John V. Luce</a> from <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Dublin" class="mw-redirect" title="Trinity College, Dublin">Trinity College, Dublin</a>, presented the results of investigations, begun in 1977, into the geology of the region.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> They compared the present geology with the landscapes and coastal features described in the <i>Iliad</i> and other classical sources, notably <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Geographica" title="Geographica">Geographia</a></i>, and concluded that there is a regular consistency between the location of Schliemann's Troy and other locations such as the Greek camp, the geological evidence, descriptions of the <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topography</a> and accounts of the battle in the <i>Iliad</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-ball_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ball-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Besides the <i>Iliad</i>, there are references to Troy in the other major work attributed to Homer, the <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i>, as well as in other ancient Greek literature (such as <a href="/wiki/Aeschylus" title="Aeschylus">Aeschylus</a>'s <a href="/wiki/The_Oresteia" class="mw-redirect" title="The Oresteia">Oresteia</a>). The Homeric legend of Troy was elaborated by the Roman poet <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Aeneid" title="Aeneid">Aeneid</a></i>. The Greeks and Romans took for a fact the <a href="/wiki/Historicity_of_the_Iliad" class="mw-redirect" title="Historicity of the Iliad">historicity of the Trojan War</a> and the identity of Homeric Troy with the site in Anatolia. <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, for example, visited the site in 334&#160;BC and there made sacrifices at tombs associated with the Homeric heroes <a href="/wiki/Achilles" title="Achilles">Achilles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patroclus" title="Patroclus">Patroclus</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Piri_Reis" title="Piri Reis">Piri Reis</a> book <i>Kitab-ƒ± Bahriye</i> (Book of the Sea, 1521) which details many ports and islands of the Mediterranean, the description of the island called Tenedos mentions Troy and it ruins, lying on the shore opposite of the island.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1995 discovery of a <a href="/wiki/Luwian" class="mw-redirect" title="Luwian">Luwian</a> biconvex seal at Troy VII sparked heated debate over the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_language" title="Trojan language">language that was spoken in Homeric Troy</a>. Frank Starke of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_T%C3%BCbingen" title="University of T√ºbingen">University of T√ºbingen</a> recently demonstrated that the name of <a href="/wiki/Priam" title="Priam">Priam</a>, king of Troy at the time of the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_War" title="Trojan War">Trojan War</a>, is connected to the Luwian compound <i>Priimuua</i>, which means "exceptionally courageous".<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> "The certainty is growing that Wilusa/Troy belonged to the greater Luwian-speaking community," although it is not entirely clear whether Luwian was primarily the official language or in daily colloquial use.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Search_for_Troy">Search for Troy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Search for Troy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Priam%27s_treasure.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Priam%27s_treasure.jpg/220px-Priam%27s_treasure.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Priam%27s_treasure.jpg/330px-Priam%27s_treasure.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/73/Priam%27s_treasure.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Priam%27s_treasure.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Priam%27s_Treasure" title="Priam&#39;s Treasure">Priam's Treasure</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Schliemann" title="Heinrich Schliemann">Heinrich Schliemann</a> claimed to have found at Troy</div></div></div>
<p>With the rise of critical history, Troy and the Trojan War were, for a long time, consigned to the realms of legend. However, the true location of ancient Troy had from <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical</a> times remained the subject of interest and speculation.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Troad" title="Troad">Troad</a> peninsula was anticipated to be the location. Early modern travellers in the 16th and 17th centuries, including <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Belon" title="Pierre Belon">Pierre Belon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pietro_Della_Valle" title="Pietro Della Valle">Pietro Della Valle</a>, had identified Troy with <a href="/wiki/Alexandria_Troas" title="Alexandria Troas">Alexandria Troas</a>, a ruined town approximately 20&#160;km south of the currently accepted location.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchliemann1881184_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchliemann1881184-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In the late 18th century, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Baptiste_LeChevalier" title="Jean Baptiste LeChevalier">Jean Baptiste LeChevalier</a> had identified a location near the village of <a href="https://tr.wikipedia.org/wiki/P%C4%B1narba%C5%9F%C4%B1,_Ezine" class="extiw" title="tr:Pƒ±narba≈üƒ±, Ezine">Pƒ±narba≈üƒ±, Ezine</a> as the site of Troy, a mound approximately 5&#160;km south of the currently accepted location. LeChavalier's location, published in his <i>Voyage de la Troade</i>, was the most commonly accepted theory for almost a century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchliemann1881184‚Äì191_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchliemann1881184‚Äì191-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1822, the Scottish journalist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Maclaren" title="Charles Maclaren">Charles Maclaren</a> was the first to identify with confidence the position of the city as it is now known.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchliemann1881189_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchliemann1881189-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1866, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Calvert" title="Frank Calvert">Frank Calvert</a>, the brother of the United States' <a href="/wiki/Consul" title="Consul">consular agent</a> in the region, made extensive surveys and published in scholarly journals his identification of the hill of New Ilium (which was on farmland owned by his family) on the same site. The hill, near the city of <a href="/wiki/%C3%87anakkale" title="√áanakkale">√áanakkale</a>, was known as Hisarlik.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Schliemann">Schliemann</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Schliemann">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1868, German archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Schliemann" title="Heinrich Schliemann">Heinrich Schliemann</a> visited Calvert and secured permission to excavate <a href="/wiki/Hisarlik" title="Hisarlik">Hisarlik</a>. In 1871‚Äì73 and 1878‚Äì79, he excavated the hill and discovered the ruins of a series of ancient cities dating from the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> to the Roman period. Schliemann declared one of these cities‚Äîat first Troy I, later Troy II‚Äîto be the city of Troy, and this identification was widely accepted at that time. Schliemann's finds at Hisarlik have become known as <a href="/wiki/Priam%27s_Treasure" title="Priam&#39;s Treasure">Priam's Treasure</a>. They were acquired from him by the <a href="/wiki/Museum_Island" title="Museum Island">Berlin</a> museums, but significant doubts about their authenticity persist.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Troja-Blick-Schliemanngraben.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Troja-Blick-Schliemanngraben.jpg/220px-Troja-Blick-Schliemanngraben.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Troja-Blick-Schliemanngraben.jpg/330px-Troja-Blick-Schliemanngraben.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Troja-Blick-Schliemanngraben.jpg/440px-Troja-Blick-Schliemanngraben.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1152" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Troja-Blick-Schliemanngraben.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The view from Hisarlƒ±k across the plain of Ilium to the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a></div></div></div>
<p>Schliemann became interested in digging at the mound of Hisarlik at the persuasion of <a href="/wiki/Frank_Calvert" title="Frank Calvert">Frank Calvert</a>. The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">British</a> diplomat, considered a pioneer for the contributions he made to the archaeology of Troy, spent more than 60 years in the Troad (modern day <a href="/wiki/Biga,_%C3%87anakkale" title="Biga, √áanakkale">Biga</a> peninsula, Turkey) conducting field work.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> As Calvert was a principal authority on field archaeology in the region, his findings supplied evidence that Homeric Troy might exist in the hill, and played a major role in directing Heinrich Schliemann to dig at the Hisarlik.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> However, Schliemann downplayed his collaboration with Calvert when taking credit for the findings, such that Susan Heuek Allen recently described Schliemann as a "relentlessly self-promoting amateur archaeologist".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen1999From_introductory_&#91;&#91;blurb&#93;&#93;&amp;nbsp;‚Äì_not_in_book_itself_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen1999From_introductory_[[blurb]]&amp;nbsp;‚Äì_not_in_book_itself-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Schliemann's excavations were condemned by later archaeologists as having destroyed the main layers of the real Troy. <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_W._Harl" title="Kenneth W. Harl">Kenneth W. Harl</a> in the Teaching Company's <i>Great Ancient Civilizations of Asia Minor</i> lecture series sarcastically claims that Schliemann's excavations were carried out with such rough methods that he did to Troy what the Achaeans had been unable to do: destroy and level the city walls completely to the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Other scholars agree that the damage caused to the site is irreparable.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="D.C3.B6rpfeld_and_Blegen"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="D√∂rpfeld_and_Blegen">D√∂rpfeld and Blegen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: D√∂rpfeld and Blegen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After Schliemann, the site was further excavated under the direction of <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_D%C3%B6rpfeld" title="Wilhelm D√∂rpfeld">Wilhelm D√∂rpfeld</a> <span class="nowrap">(1893‚Äì94)</span> and later <a href="/wiki/Carl_Blegen" title="Carl Blegen">Carl Blegen</a> (1932‚Äì38).
<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (April 2013)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
These excavations have shown that there were at least nine cities built, one on top of the other, at this site. In his research, Blegen came to a conclusion that Troy's nine levels could be further divided into forty-six sublevels.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Korfmann">Korfmann</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Korfmann">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1988, excavations were resumed by a team from the University of T√ºbingen and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cincinnati" title="University of Cincinnati">University of Cincinnati</a> under the direction of Professor <a href="/wiki/Manfred_Korfmann" title="Manfred Korfmann">Manfred Korfmann</a>, with Professor <a href="/wiki/C._Brian_Rose" title="C. Brian Rose">Brian Rose</a> overseeing Post-Bronze Age (Greek, Roman, Byzantine) excavation along the coast of the Aegean Sea at the Bay of Troy. Possible evidence of a battle was found in the form of bronze arrowheads and fire-damaged human remains buried in layers dated to the early 12th century BC. The question of Troy's status in the Bronze-Age world has been the subject of a sometimes acerbic debate between Korfmann and the T√ºbingen historian <a href="/wiki/Frank_Kolb" title="Frank Kolb">Frank Kolb</a> in 2001‚Äì2002.
</p><p>Korfmann proposed that the location of the city (close to the <a href="/wiki/Dardanelles" title="Dardanelles">Dardanelles</a>) indicated a commercially oriented city that would have been at the center of a vibrant trade between the Black Sea, Aegean, Anatolian and Eastern Mediterranean regions. Kolb disputed this thesis, calling it "unfounded" in a 2004 paper. He argues that archaeological evidence shows that economic trade during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Bronze_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Bronze Age">Late Bronze Age</a> was quite limited in the Aegean region compared with later periods in antiquity. On the other hand, the Eastern Mediterranean economy was more active during this time, allowing for commercial cities to develop only in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>. Kolb also noted the lack of evidence for trade with the <a href="/wiki/Hittite_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Hittite Empire">Hittite Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 1993, following a magnetic imaging survey of the fields below the fort, a deep ditch was located and excavated among the ruins of a later Greek and Roman city. Remains found in the ditch were dated to the late Bronze Age, the alleged time of Homeric Troy. It is claimed by Korfmann that the ditch may have once marked the outer defences of a much larger city than had previously been suspected. The latter city has been dated by his team to about 1250&#160;BC, and it has been also suggested‚Äîbased on recent archeological evidence uncovered by Professor Manfred Korfmann's team‚Äîthat this was indeed the Homeric city of Troy.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_developments">Recent developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Recent developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The archaeological site of Troy was added to the <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage" class="mw-redirect" title="UNESCO World Heritage">UNESCO World Heritage</a> list in 1998.
</p><p>In summer 2006, the excavations continued under the direction of Korfmann's colleague Ernst Pernicka, with a new digging permit.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013, an international team made up of cross-disciplinary experts led by William Aylward, an archaeologist at the University of Wisconsin-Madison, was to carry out new excavations. This activity was to be conducted under the auspices of <a href="/wiki/%C3%87anakkale_Onsekiz_Mart_University" title="√áanakkale Onsekiz Mart University">√áanakkale Onsekiz Mart University</a> and was to use the new technique of "molecular archaeology".<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> A few days before the Wisconsin team was to leave, Turkey cancelled about 100 excavation permits, including Wisconsin's.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2014, it was announced that a new excavation would take place to be sponsored by a private company and carried out by √áanakkale Onsekiz Mart University. This will be the first Turkish team to excavate and is planned as a 12-month excavation led by associate professor R√ºstem Aslan. The University's rector stated that "Pieces unearthed in Troy will contribute to √áanakkale‚Äôs culture and tourism. Maybe it will become one of Turkey‚Äôs most important frequented historical places.‚Äù<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fortifications_of_the_city">Fortifications of the city</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Fortifications of the city">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The walls of Troy, first erected in the Bronze Age between 3000 and 2600 BC, are its main defense. The remains of the walls have been studied through the aforementioned excavations that shed light onto the historical city itself and the mythological implications as the walls protected the citadel during the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_War" title="Trojan War">Trojan War</a>. The fortifications display the importance of defense to the Trojans and how warfare is a prominent issue for ancient cities.
</p><p>The walls surround the city, extending for several hundred meters, and at the time they were built they were over 17 <a href="/wiki/Foot_(unit)" title="Foot (unit)">feet</a> (5.2&#160;<a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">m</a>) tall.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> They were made of limestone, with watchtowers and brick ramparts, or elevated mounds that served as protective barriers.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Throughout all of the phases, the walls served as the largest fortification to protect the Trojans against their enemies. Defense mechanisms like the walls of Troy shed light on the larger topic of warfare in ancient times, which was a significant issue in Ancient Greece and in nearby locations such as Asia Minor.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_Troy_uncovered">Historical Troy uncovered</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Historical Troy uncovered">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Plan_Troy-Hisarlik-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Plan_Troy-Hisarlik-en.svg/220px-Plan_Troy-Hisarlik-en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Plan_Troy-Hisarlik-en.svg/330px-Plan_Troy-Hisarlik-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Plan_Troy-Hisarlik-en.svg/440px-Plan_Troy-Hisarlik-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="1155" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Plan_Troy-Hisarlik-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Archeological plan of the Hisarlik citadel</div></div></div>
<p>When Troy was destroyed each time, the citizens would build upon the previous settlement, causing the layers to pile on top of one another.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The layers of ruins in the citadel at <a href="/wiki/Hisarlik" title="Hisarlik">Hisarlik</a> are numbered Troy I&#160;&#8211;&#32;Troy IX, with various subdivisions:<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Troy I 3000‚Äì2600 BC (Western Anatolian <a href="/wiki/Early_Bronze_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Bronze Age">EB</a> 1)</li>
<li>Troy II 2600‚Äì2250 BC (Western Anatolian EB 2)</li>
<li>Troy III 2250‚Äì2100 BC (Western Anatolian EB 3 [early])</li>
<li>Troy IV 2100‚Äì1950 BC (Western Anatolian EB 3 [middle])</li>
<li>Troy V: 20th‚Äì18th centuries BC (Western Anatolian EB 3 [late])</li>
<li>Troy VI: 17th‚Äì15th centuries BC</li>
<li>Troy VIh: late Bronze Age, 14th century BC</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Troy_VII" title="Troy VII">Troy VIIa</a>: c. 1300‚Äì1190 BC, most likely setting for Homer's story</li>
<li>Troy VIIb<sub>1</sub>: 12th century BC</li>
<li>Troy VIIb<sub>2</sub>: 11th century BC</li>
<li>Troy VIIb<sub>3</sub>: until c. 950 BC</li>
<li>Troy VIII: c. 700‚Äì85 BC</li>
<li>Troy IX: 85 BC‚Äìc. AD 500</li></ul>
<h3><span id="Troy_I.E2.80.93V"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Troy_I‚ÄìV">Troy I‚ÄìV</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Troy I‚ÄìV">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first city on the site was founded in the 3rd millennium BC. During the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, the site seems to have been a flourishing mercantile city, since its location allowed for complete control of the <a href="/wiki/Dardanelles" title="Dardanelles">Dardanelles</a>, through which every merchant ship from the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a> heading for the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> had to pass. Cities to the east of Troy were destroyed, and although Troy was not burned, the next period shows a change of culture indicating a new people had taken over Troy.<sup id="cite_ref-Mellaart_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mellaart-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The first phase of the city is characterized by a smaller citadel, around 300&#160;ft in diameter, with 20 rectangular houses surrounded by massive walls, towers, and gateways.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Troy II doubled in size and had a lower town and the upper citadel, with the walls protecting the upper acropolis which housed the <a href="/wiki/Megaron" title="Megaron">megaron</a>-style palace for the king.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The second phase was destroyed by a large fire, but the Trojans rebuilt, creating a fortified citadel larger than Troy II, but which had smaller and more condensed houses, suggesting an economic decline.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_38-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> This trend of making a larger circuit, or extent of the walls, continued with each rebuild, for Troy III, IV, and V. Therefore, even in the face of economic troubles, the walls remained as elaborate as before, indicating their focus on defense and protection.
</p>
<h4><span id="Schliemann.27s_Troy_II"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Schliemann's_Troy_II">Schliemann's Troy II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Schliemann&#039;s Troy II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>When Schliemann came across Troy II, in 1871, he believed he had found Homer's city. Schliemann and his team unearthed a large feature he dubbed the Scaean Gate, a western gate unlike the three previously found leading to the Pergamos.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> This gate, as he describes, was the gate that Homer had featured. As Schliemann states in his publication <i>Troja</i>:
"I have proved that in a remote antiquity there was in the plain of Troy a large city, destroyed of old by a fearful catastrophe, which had on the hill of Hisarlƒ±k only its Acropolis with its temples and a few other large edifices, southerly, and westerly direction on the site of the later Ilium; and that, consequently, this city answers perfectly to the Homeric description of the sacred site of Ilios."<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Troy_VI_and_VII">Troy VI and VII</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Troy VI and VII">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Troy_VII" title="Troy VII">Troy VII</a></div>
<p>Troy VI was destroyed around 1250 BC, probably by an <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">earthquake</a>. Only a single arrowhead was found in this layer, and no remains of bodies. However, the town quickly recovered and was rebuilt in a layout that was more orderly. This rebuild continued the trend of having a heavily fortified citadel to preserve the outer rim of the city in the face of earthquakes and sieges of the central city.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Troy VI can be characterized by the construction of the pillars at the south gate. There appears to be no structural use for the pillars. The pillars have an altar-like base and an impressive magnitude. This provides some clues, and they most likely were used as a symbol for the religious cults of the city.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Another characterizing feature of Troy VI is the tightly packed housing near the Citadel and construction of many cobble streets. Although only few homes could be uncovered, this is due to reconstruction of Troy VIIa over the tops of them.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Also, discovered in 1890, in this layer of Troy VI was Mycenaean pottery. This pottery suggests that during Troy IV, the Trojans still had trade with the Greeks and the Aegean. Furthermore, there were cremation burials discovered 400m south of the citadel wall. This provided evidence of a small lower city south of the Hellenistic city walls. Although the size of this city is unknown due to erosion and regular building activities, there is significant evidence that was uncovered by Blegen in 1953 during an excavation of the site. This evidence included settlements just above bedrock and a ditch thought to be used for defense. Furthermore, the small settlement itself, south of the wall, could have also been used as an obstacle to defend the main city walls and the citadel.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The topic still under debate is whether Troy was primarily an Anatolian-oriented or Aegean-oriented metropolis. While it is true that the city would have had a presence in the Aegean,  pottery finds and architecture strongly hint at an Anatolian orientation. Only about one percent of the pottery discovered during excavation of Troy VI was Mycenaean. The large walls and gates of the city are closely related to many other Anatolian designs. Furthermore, the practice of cremation is Anatolian. Cremation is never seen in the Mycenaean world. Anatolian hieroglyphic writing along with bronze seals marked with Anatolian hieroglyphic Luwian were also uncovered in 1995. These seals have been seen in approximately 20 other Anatolian and Syrian cities from the time (1280 - 1175 BC).<sup id="cite_ref-Korfmann_1998_369_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korfmann_1998_369-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Still, Troy VI was dominated by long distance trade. Troy VI during the height of its establishment held anywhere from 5,000 to 10,000 people. At its time, Troy would have been a large and significant city.<sup id="cite_ref-Korfmann_1998_369_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korfmann_1998_369-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The location of Troy was extremely practical in the Early Bronze Age (2000‚Äì1500 BC). It acted as a middle ground for long distance trade with regions as far distant as Afghanistan, the Persian Gulf, the Baltic region, Egypt, and the western Mediterranean in the Middle and Late Bronze Ages.  Earlier trade connections during the Early and Middle Bronze Ages provided Troy VI with favorable power in the long distance trade industry of the region. The amount of objects thought to be going through Troy VI would have been quite large, obtaining metals from the east and various objects from the west including perfumes and oils. This is known due to the findings of hundreds of shipwrecks off the Turkish coast. Found in these ships was an abundance of goods. Some of these ships carried over 15 tons in goods. The goods discovered in these wrecks included copper ingots, tin ingots, glass ingots, bronze tools and weapons, ebony and ivory, ostrich egg shells, jewelry and large amounts of pottery from across the Mediterranean.<sup id="cite_ref-Kolb_577‚Äì613_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kolb_577‚Äì613-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> &#160;
</p><p>There have been 210 shipwrecks discovered in the Mediterranean from the Bronze Age. Of these 210, 63 were discovered of Turkish coastline. This provides a great deal of evidence for Troy VI being a prominent trading center for the region. But, the evidence at the site of Troy itself is minimal. Looking at the layers of Troy VI, we discover that there is little documentation of the excavation of this layer, and little documentation of the goods discovered in this layer. We also know that there were few trading centers during the Late Bronze Age. This is due to the low volume of trade during this period. The trading centers would have most likely been directly along trade routes. Troy is just north of most major long-distance trade routes. It may be unfair to classify Troy VI as a trading center but we do know that Troy VI was a prominent metropolis that did contribute to the trade of the region.<sup id="cite_ref-Kolb_577‚Äì613_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kolb_577‚Äì613-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Troy VIIa can be highlighted by most of the population of Troy moving within the walls of the Citadel. This is most likely due to the threat from the Mycenaeans.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Troy VI is believed to have been destroyed by an earthquake. This would not have been uncommon. Earthquakes are common throughout the region. Troy VIIa is believed to be built over the ruined Troy VI, which makes the excavation process of Troy difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Troy VIIa, which has been dated to the mid-to-late-13th century BC, is the most often cited candidate for the Troy of Homer. Troy VIIa appears to have been destroyed by war.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The evidence of fire and slaughter around 1184 BC, which brought Troy VIIa to a close, led to this phase being identified with the city besieged by the Greeks during the Trojan War. This was immortalized in the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> written by Homer.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Calvert.27s_Thousand-Year_Gap"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Calvert's_Thousand-Year_Gap">Calvert's Thousand-Year Gap</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Calvert&#039;s Thousand-Year Gap">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Initially, the layers of Troy VI and VII were overlooked entirely, because Schliemann favoured the burnt city of Troy II. It was not until the need to close "Calvert's Thousand Year Gap" arose‚Äîfrom D√∂rpfeld's discovery of Troy VI‚Äîthat archaeology turned away from Schliemann's Troy and began working towards finding Homeric Troy once more.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>"Calvert's Thousand Year Gap" (1800‚Äì800 BC) was a period not accounted for by Schliemann's archaeology and thus constituted a hole in the Trojan timeline. In Homer's description of the city, a section of one side of the wall is said to be weaker than the rest.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> During his excavation of more than three hundred yards of the wall, D√∂rpfeld came across a section very closely resembling the Homeric description of the weaker section.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> D√∂rpfeld was convinced he had found the walls of Homer's city, and now he would excavate the city itself. Within the walls of this stratum (Troy VI), much <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean</a> pottery dating from <a href="/wiki/Late_Helladic" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Helladic">Late Helladic</a> (LH) periods III A and III B (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>1400‚Äì<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>1200 BC) was uncovered, suggesting a relation between the Trojans and Mycenaeans. The great tower along the walls seemed likely to be the "Great Tower of Ilios".<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The evidence seemed to indicate that D√∂rpfeld had stumbled upon Ilios, the city of Homer's epics. Schliemann himself had conceded that Troy VI was more likely to be the Homeric city, but he never published anything stating so.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The only counter-argument, confirmed initially by D√∂rpfeld (who was as passionate as Schliemann about finding Troy), was that the city appeared to have been destroyed by an earthquake, not by men.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> There was little doubt that this was the Troy of which the Mycenaeans would have known.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Troy_VIII">Troy VIII</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Troy VIII">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 480 BC, the Persian king <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I_of_Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Xerxes I of Persia">Xerxes</a> sacrificed 1,000 cattle at the sanctuary of Athena Ilias while marching through the Hellespontine region towards Greece.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Following the Persian defeat in 480‚Äì479, Ilion and its territory became part of the continental possessions of <a href="/wiki/Mytilene" title="Mytilene">Mytilene</a> and remained under Mytilenaean control until the unsuccessful <a href="/wiki/Mytilenean_revolt" title="Mytilenean revolt">Mytilenean revolt</a> in 428‚Äì427. Athens liberated the so-called Actaean cities including Ilion and enrolled these communities in the <a href="/wiki/Delian_League" title="Delian League">Delian League</a>. Athenian influence in the Hellespont waned following the oligarchic coup of 411, and in that year the Spartan general Mindaros emulated Xerxes by likewise sacrificing to Athena Ilias.<sup id="cite_ref-ilium_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ilium-1">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> From c. 410‚Äì399, Ilion was within the sphere of influence of the local dynasts at <a href="/wiki/Lampsacus" title="Lampsacus">Lampsacus</a> (Zenis, his wife Mania, and the usurper Meidias) who administered the region on behalf of the Persian satrap <a href="/wiki/Pharnabazos_II,_Satrap_of_Phrygia" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharnabazos II, Satrap of Phrygia">Pharnabazus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ilium_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ilium-1">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 399, the Spartan general <a href="/wiki/Dercylidas" title="Dercylidas">Dercylidas</a> expelled the Greek garrison at Ilion who were controlling the city on behalf of the Lampsacene dynasts during a campaign which rolled back Persian influence throughout the Troad. Ilion remained outside the control of the Persian satrapal administration at <a href="/wiki/Dascylium" title="Dascylium">Dascylium</a> until the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Antalcidas" title="Peace of Antalcidas">Peace of Antalcidas</a> in 387‚Äì386. In this period of renewed Persian control c. 387‚Äì367, a statue of <a href="/wiki/Ariobarzanes_of_Phrygia" title="Ariobarzanes of Phrygia">Ariobarzanes</a>, the satrap of <a href="/wiki/Hellespontine_Phrygia" title="Hellespontine Phrygia">Hellespontine Phrygia</a>, was erected in front of the temple of Athena Ilias.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> In 360‚Äì359 the city was briefly controlled by <a href="/wiki/Charidemus" title="Charidemus">Charidemus</a> of <a href="/wiki/Oreus" title="Oreus">Oreus</a>, a Euboean mercenary leader who occasionally worked for the Athenians.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In 359, he was expelled by the Athenian Menelaos son of Arrabaios, whom the Ilians honoured with a grant of <a href="/wiki/Proxeny" title="Proxeny">proxeny</a>‚Äîthis is recorded in the earliest civic decree to survive from Ilion.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In May 334 <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> crossed the Hellespont and came to the city, where he visited the temple of Athena Ilias, made sacrifices at the tombs of the Homeric heroes, and made the city free and exempt from taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> According to the so-called 'Last Plans' of Alexander which became known after his death in June 323, he had planned to rebuild the temple of Athena Ilias on a scale that would have surpassed every other temple in the known world.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Antigonus_Monophthalmus" class="mw-redirect" title="Antigonus Monophthalmus">Antigonus Monophthalmus</a> took control of the Troad in 311 and created the new city of <a href="/wiki/Alexandria_Troas" title="Alexandria Troas">Antigoneia Troas</a> which was a <a href="/wiki/Synoikism" class="mw-redirect" title="Synoikism">synoikism</a> of the cities of <a href="/wiki/Skepsis" class="mw-redirect" title="Skepsis">Skepsis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cebren" title="Cebren">Kebren</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neandreia" title="Neandreia">Neandreia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamaxitus" title="Hamaxitus">Hamaxitos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larisa_(Troad)" title="Larisa (Troad)">Larisa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kolonai" title="Kolonai">Kolonai</a>. In c. 311‚Äì306 the <i><a href="/wiki/Koinon" title="Koinon">koinon</a> </i>of Athena Ilias was founded from the remaining cities in the Troad and along the Asian coast of the <a href="/wiki/Dardanelles" title="Dardanelles">Dardanelles</a> and soon after succeeded in securing a guarantee from Antigonus that he would respect their autonomy and freedom (he had not respected the autonomy of the cities which were synoikized to create Antigoneia).<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The <i>koinon </i>continued to function until at least the 1st century AD and primarily consisted of cities from the Troad, although for a time in the second half of the 3rd century it also included <a href="/wiki/Myrlea" class="mw-redirect" title="Myrlea">Myrlea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chalcedon" title="Chalcedon">Chalcedon</a> from the eastern <a href="/wiki/Propontis" class="mw-redirect" title="Propontis">Propontis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The governing body of the <i>koinon </i>was the <i>synedrion</i> on which each city was represented by two delegates. The day-to-day running of the <i>synedrion</i>, especially in relation to its finances, was left to a college of five <i>agonothetai</i>, on which no city ever had more than one representative. This system of equal (rather than proportional) representation ensured that no one city could politically dominate the <i>koinon</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The primary purpose of the <i>koinon </i>was to organize the annual Panathenaia festival which was held at the sanctuary of Athena Ilias. The festival brought huge numbers of pilgrims to Ilion for the duration of the festival as well as creating an enormous market (the <i>panegyris</i>) which attracted traders from across the region.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In addition, the <i>koinon </i>financed new building projects at Ilion, for example a new theatre c. 306 and the expansion of the sanctuary and temple of Athena Ilias in the 3rd century, in order to make the city a suitable venue for such a large festival.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the period 302‚Äì281, Ilion and the Troad were part of the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Lysimachus" title="Lysimachus">Lysimachus</a>, who during this time helped Ilion synoikize several nearby communities, thus expanding the city's population and territory.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;note 3&#93;</a></sup> Lysimachus was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Corupedium" title="Battle of Corupedium">Battle of Corupedium</a> in February 281 by <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_I_Nikator" class="mw-redirect" title="Seleucus I Nikator">Seleucus I Nikator</a>, thus handing the Seleucid kingdom control of <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, and in August or September of 281 when Seleucus passed through the Troad on his way to <a href="/wiki/Lysimachia_(Thrace)" title="Lysimachia (Thrace)">Lysimachia</a> in the nearby Thracian Chersonese Ilion passed a decree in honour of him, indicating the city's new loyalties.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In September Seleucus was assassinated at Lysimachia by <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_Keraunos" title="Ptolemy Keraunos">Ptolemy Keraunos</a>, making his successor, <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_I_Soter" title="Antiochus I Soter">Antiochus I Soter</a>, the new king. In 280 or soon after Ilion passed a long decree lavishly honouring Antiochus in order to cement their relationship with him.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;note 4&#93;</a></sup> During this period Ilion still lacked proper city walls except for the crumbling Troy VI fortifications around the citadel, and in 278 during the <a href="/wiki/Gallic_invasion_of_the_Balkans" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallic invasion of the Balkans">Gallic invasion</a> the city was easily sacked.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Ilion enjoyed a close relationship with Antiochus for the rest of his reign: for example, in 274 Antiochus granted land to his friend Aristodikides of Assos which for tax purposes was to be attached to the territory of Ilion, and c. 275‚Äì269 Ilion passed a decree in honour of Metrodoros of Amphipolis who had successfully treated the king for a wound he received in battle.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Troy_IX">Troy IX</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Troy IX">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tetradrachm_from_Troy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Tetradrachm_from_Troy.jpg/220px-Tetradrachm_from_Troy.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="103" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Tetradrachm_from_Troy.jpg/330px-Tetradrachm_from_Troy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Tetradrachm_from_Troy.jpg/440px-Tetradrachm_from_Troy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="351" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tetradrachm_from_Troy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silver <a href="/wiki/Tetradrachm" title="Tetradrachm">tetradrachm</a> from Troy with head of <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a>, c. 165‚Äì150&#160;BC</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hadrianic_Odeon_in_Troy_IX_(Ilion).Turkey_(3).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Hadrianic_Odeon_in_Troy_IX_%28Ilion%29.Turkey_%283%29.jpg/220px-Hadrianic_Odeon_in_Troy_IX_%28Ilion%29.Turkey_%283%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Hadrianic_Odeon_in_Troy_IX_%28Ilion%29.Turkey_%283%29.jpg/330px-Hadrianic_Odeon_in_Troy_IX_%28Ilion%29.Turkey_%283%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Hadrianic_Odeon_in_Troy_IX_%28Ilion%29.Turkey_%283%29.jpg/440px-Hadrianic_Odeon_in_Troy_IX_%28Ilion%29.Turkey_%283%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="3216" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hadrianic_Odeon_in_Troy_IX_(Ilion).Turkey_(3).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Odeon_(building)" title="Odeon (building)">odeon</a> dates to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman</a> Troy IX and was renovated by <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a> in 124 AD.</div></div></div>
<p>The city was destroyed by <a href="/wiki/Sulla" title="Sulla">Sulla</a>'s rival, the Roman general <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Flavius_Fimbria" title="Gaius Flavius Fimbria">Fimbria</a>, in 85 BC following an eleven-day siege.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Later that year when Sulla had defeated Fimbria he bestowed benefactions on Ilion for its loyalty which helped with the city's rebuilding. Ilion reciprocated this act of generosity by instituting a new civic calendar which took 85 BC as its first year.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> However, the city remained in financial distress for several decades, despite its favoured status with Rome. In the 80s BC, Roman <i>publicani</i> illegally levied taxes on the sacred estates of Athena Ilias and the city was required to call on <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Julius_Caesar_(consul_64_BC)" title="Lucius Julius Caesar (consul 64 BC)">L. Julius Caesar</a> for restitution; while in 80 BC, the city suffered an attack by pirates.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In 77 BC the costs of running the annual festival of the <i>koinon</i> of Athena Ilias became too pressing for both Ilion and the other members of the <i>koinon</i> and L. Julius Caesar was once again required to arbitrate, this time reforming the festival so that it would be less of a financial burden.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> In 74 BC the Ilians once again demonstrated their loyalty to Rome by siding with the Roman general <a href="/wiki/Lucullus" title="Lucullus">Lucullus</a> against Mithridates VI.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Following the final defeat of Mithridates in 63‚Äì62, <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a> rewarded the city's loyalty by becoming the benefactor of Ilion and patron of Athena Ilias.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> In 48 BC, <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> likewise bestowed benefactions on the city, recalling the city's loyalty during the Mithridatic Wars, the city's connection with his cousin L. Julius Caesar, and the family's claim that they were ultimately descended from Venus through the Trojan prince <a href="/wiki/Aeneas" title="Aeneas">Aeneas</a> and therefore shared kinship with the Ilians.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 20 BC, the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> visited Ilion and stayed in the house of a leading citizen, Melanippides son of Euthydikos.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> As a result of his visit, he also financed the restoration and rebuilding of the sanctuary of Athena Ilias, the <a href="/wiki/Bouleuterion" title="Bouleuterion">bouleuterion</a> (council house) and the theatre. Soon after work on the theatre was completed in 12‚Äì11 BC, Melanippides dedicated a statue of Augustus in the theatre to record this benefaction.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Classical_Ilium_.28Ilion.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_Ilium_(Ilion)">Classical Ilium (Ilion)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Classical Ilium (Ilion)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A new city called <b>Ilium</b> (from Greek Ilion) was founded on the site in the reign of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Emperor">Roman Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a>. It flourished until the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, became a bishopric in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">Roman province</a> <a href="/wiki/Hellespontus_(province)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellespontus (province)">Hellespontus</a> (civil <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Asia" title="Diocese of Asia">Diocese of Asia</a>), but declined gradually in the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine era</a>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecclesiastical_history">Ecclesiastical history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Ecclesiastical history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>No later than the 4th century, it was a <a href="/wiki/Suffragan" class="mw-redirect" title="Suffragan">suffragan</a> of the provincial capital's Metropolitan <a href="/wiki/Archdiocese_of_Cyzicus" class="mw-redirect" title="Archdiocese of Cyzicus">Archdiocese of Cyzicus</a>, in the sway of the <a href="/wiki/Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" class="mw-redirect" title="Patriarchate of Constantinople">Patriarchate of Constantinople</a>.
Several bishops are historically documented:
</p>
<ul><li>Orion attended the <a href="/wiki/First_Ecumenical_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="First Ecumenical Council">First Ecumenical Council</a> at <a href="/wiki/Nicaea" title="Nicaea">Nicaea</a> in 325</li>
<li>Leucadius was among the schismatic group of <a href="/wiki/Arian" class="mw-redirect" title="Arian">Arian</a> heretical bishops abandoning the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Sardica" class="mw-redirect" title="Council of Sardica">Council of Sardica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Philippopolis" title="Council of Philippopolis">Council of Philippopolis</a> in 344 to convene their alternative 'synod'.</li>
<li>Theosebius partook in the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a> in 451.</li>
<li>Johannes participated in the second <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Constantinople" title="Second Council of Constantinople">Council of Constantinople</a> in 553.</li>
<li>Nicetas attended the <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Nicaea" title="Second Council of Nicaea">Second Council of Nicaea</a> in 787.</li>
<li>Georgius participated in the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Constantinople_(869-870)" class="mw-redirect" title="Council of Constantinople (869-870)">Council of Constantinople</a> of 869‚Äì870 which condemned Patriarch <a href="/wiki/Photios_I_of_Constantinople" title="Photios I of Constantinople">Photios of Constantinople</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Titular_see">Titular see</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Titular see">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The diocese was nominally restored no later than 1926 as Latin <a href="/wiki/Titular_bishopric" class="mw-redirect" title="Titular bishopric">Titular bishopric</a> of Ilium (Latin) / Ilio (Curiate Italian) / Ilien(sis) (Latin adjective).
</p><p>It has been vacant for decades, having had the following incumbents, so far of the fitting Episcopal (lowest) rank:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Michel_d%27Herbigny" title="Michel d&#39;Herbigny">Michel d'Herbigny</a> <a href="/wiki/Jesuit_Order" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesuit Order">S.J.</a> (1926.02.11 ‚Äì 1937.07)</li>
<li>James Maguire (1939.10.05 ‚Äì 1944.10.10)</li>
<li>Eugene Joseph McGuinness (1944.11.11 ‚Äì 1948.02.01)</li>
<li>Leo John Steck (1948.03.13 ‚Äì 1950.06.19)</li>
<li>Francesco Maria Franco (1950.07.10 ‚Äì 1968.02.07)</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_views">Alternative views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Alternative views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Location">Location</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Location">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A small minority of contemporary writers argue that Homeric Troy was not at the Hisarlik site, but elsewhere in Anatolia or outside it‚Äîe.g. in England,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pergamum" class="mw-redirect" title="Pergamum">Pergamum</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Scandinavia,<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Herzegovina" title="Herzegovina">Herzegovina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> These proposals have not been accepted by mainstream scholarship.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hittite_and_Egyptian_records">Hittite and Egyptian records</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Hittite and Egyptian records">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 1920s, the <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Swiss</a> scholar <a href="/wiki/Emil_Forrer" title="Emil Forrer">Emil Forrer</a> claimed that the placenames Wilusa and Taruisa found in <a href="/wiki/Hittite_language" title="Hittite language">Hittite</a> texts should be identified with Ilion and Troia, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> He further noted that the name of <a href="/wiki/Alaksandu" title="Alaksandu">Alaksandu</a>, a king of Wilusa mentioned in a Hittite treaty, is quite similar to Homer's <a href="/wiki/Paris_(mythology)" title="Paris (mythology)">Paris</a>, whose birthname was Alexandros. Subsequent to this, the <a href="/wiki/Tawagalawa_letter" title="Tawagalawa letter">Tawagalawa letter</a> (<a href="/wiki/Catalogue_des_Textes_Hittites" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalogue des Textes Hittites">CTH</a> 181) was found to document an unnamed Hittite king's correspondence to the king of the <a href="/wiki/Ahhiyawa" class="mw-redirect" title="Ahhiyawa">Ahhiyawa</a>, referring to an earlier "Wilusa episode" involving hostility on the part of the Ahhiyawa. The Hittite king was long held to be <a href="/wiki/Mursili_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Mursili II">Mursili II</a> (c.&#160;1321‚Äì1296), but, since the 1980s, his son <a href="/wiki/Hattusili_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Hattusili III">Hattusili III</a> (1265‚Äì1240) is commonly preferred, although his other son <a href="/wiki/Muwatalli_II" title="Muwatalli II">Muwatalli</a> (c.&#160;1296‚Äì1272) remains a possibility.
</p><p>Inscriptions of the <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">New Kingdom of Egypt</a> also record a nation T-R-S as one of the <a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">Sea Peoples</a> who attacked <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Nineteenth_dynasty_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Nineteenth dynasty of Egypt">XIX</a> and <a href="/wiki/Twentieth_dynasty_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Twentieth dynasty of Egypt">XX Dynasties</a>. An inscription at <a href="/wiki/Deir_el-Medina" title="Deir el-Medina">Deir el-Medina</a> records a victory of <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_III" title="Ramesses III">Ramesses III</a> over the Sea Peoples, including one named "Tursha" (Egyptian: [<i>twr≈°3</i>]). It is probably the same as the earlier "Teresh" (Egyptian: [<i>tr≈°.w</i>]) on the <a href="/wiki/Merneptah_Stele" title="Merneptah Stele">stele</a> commemorating <a href="/wiki/Merneptah" title="Merneptah">Merneptah</a>'s victory in a <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Libya" title="Ancient Libya">Libyan</a> campaign around 1220&#160;BC.
</p><p>These identifications were rejected by many scholars as being improbable or at least unprovable. However, <a href="/wiki/Trevor_Bryce" class="mw-redirect" title="Trevor Bryce">Trevor Bryce</a> championed them in his 1998 book <i>The Kingdom of the Hittites</i>, citing a piece of the <a href="/wiki/Manapa-Tarhunda_letter" class="mw-redirect" title="Manapa-Tarhunda letter">Manapa-Tarhunda letter</a> referring to the kingdom of Wilusa as beyond the land of the Seha River (the classical <a href="/wiki/Caicus" class="mw-redirect" title="Caicus">Caicus</a> and modern <a href="/wiki/Bak%C4%B1r%C3%A7ay" title="Bakƒ±r√ßay">Bakƒ±r√ßay</a>) and near the land of "Lazpa" (<a href="/wiki/Lesbos" title="Lesbos">Lesbos</a>). Recent evidence also adds weight to the theory that Wilusa is identical to archaeological Troy. Hittite texts mention a <a href="/wiki/Water_tunnel_(physical_infrastructure)" title="Water tunnel (physical infrastructure)">water tunnel</a> at Wilusa, and a water tunnel excavated by Korfmann, previously thought to be Roman, has been dated to around 2600&#160;BC. The identifications of Wilusa with Troy and of the Ahhiyawa with Homer's <a href="/wiki/Achaeans_(Homer)" title="Achaeans (Homer)">Achaeans</a> remain somewhat controversial but gained enough popularity during the 1990s to be considered majority opinion. That agrees with <a href="/wiki/Metrical_foot" class="mw-redirect" title="Metrical foot">metrical</a> evidence in the <a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a> that the name ·æΩŒôŒªŒπŒøŒΩ (Ilion) for Troy was formerly œúŒπŒªŒπŒøŒΩ (Wilion) with a <a href="/wiki/Digamma" title="Digamma">digamma</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_later_legend">In later legend</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Troy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: In later legend">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Such was the fame of the <a href="/wiki/Epic_Cycle" title="Epic Cycle">Epic Cycle</a> in Roman and Medieval times that it was built upon to provide a starting point for various <a href="/wiki/Founding_myth" class="mw-redirect" title="Founding myth">founding myths</a> of national origins. The most influential, <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Aeneid" title="Aeneid">Aeneid</a></i>, traces the journeys of the Trojan prince <a href="/wiki/Aeneas" title="Aeneas">Aeneas</a>, supposed ancestor of the <a href="/wiki/Romulus_and_Remus" title="Romulus and Remus">founders of Rome</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Julio-Claudian_dynasty" title="Julio-Claudian dynasty">Julio-Claudian dynasty</a>. In a later era, the heroes of Troy, both those noted in Homer and those invented for the purpose, often continued to appear in the origin stories of the nations of Early Medieval Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-huppert_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huppert-90">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Roman_de_Troie" title="Roman de Troie">Roman de Troie</a></i> was common cultural ground for European dynasties,<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> as a Trojan pedigree was both gloriously ancient and established an equality with the ruling class of Rome. A Trojan pedigree could justify the occupation of parts of Rome's former territories.<sup id="cite_ref-huppert_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huppert-90">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Dionysius_of_Halicarnassus" title="Dionysius of Halicarnassus">Dionysius of Halicarnassus</a> writes that the Trojans, were Greek people who were originally from the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Peloponnesus">Peloponnesus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On that basis, the Franks filled the lacunae of their legendary origins with Trojan and pseudo-Trojan names: in <a href="/wiki/Fredegar" class="mw-redirect" title="Fredegar">Fredegar</a>'s 7th-century chronicle of Frankish history, Priam appears as the first king of the Franks.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed (April 2013)">full citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Trojan origin of France was such an established article of faith that in 1714, the learned <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Fr%C3%A9ret" title="Nicolas Fr√©ret">Nicolas Fr√©ret</a> was <a href="/wiki/Bastille" title="Bastille">Bastilled</a> for showing through historical criticism that the Franks had been Germanic, a sore point counter to <a href="/wiki/Valois_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Valois dynasty">Valois</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bourbon_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Bourbon dynasty">Bourbon</a> propaganda.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed (April 2013)">full citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In similar manner, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_of_Monmouth" title="Geoffrey of Monmouth">Geoffrey of Monmouth</a> reworked earlier material such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Brittonum" title="Historia Brittonum">Historia Brittonum</a></i> to trace the <a href="/wiki/List_of_legendary_kings_of_Britain" title="List of legendary kings of Britain">legendary kings of the Britons</a> from a supposed descendant of <a href="/wiki/Aeneas" title="Aeneas">Aeneas</a> called <a href="/wiki/Brutus_of_Troy" title="Brutus of Troy">Brutus</a>.
</p><p>Likewise, <a href="/wiki/Snorri_Sturluson" title="Snorri Sturluson">Snorri Sturluson</a>, in the prologue to his Icelandic <i><a href="/wiki/Prose_Edda" title="Prose Edda">Prose Edda</a></i>, traced the genealogy of the ancestral figures in <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse mythology</a> to characters appearing at Troy in Homer's epic, notably making <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">Thor</a> to be the son of <a href="/wiki/Memnon_(mythology)" title="Memnon (mythology)">Memnon</a>. Sturluson referred to these figures as having made a journey across Europe towards <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, setting up kingdoms as they went.
</p>
